colspan="2" class="infobox-below"><div class="plainlist" style="line-height:1.3em; padding-top:0.4em; border-top: 1px solid #aaa"><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Media_of_the_United_Kingdom" Kingdom">List of newspapers</a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><i><b>The Guardian</b></i> is a British daily newspaper. It was founded in 1821 as <i>The Manchester Guardian</i>, and changed its name in 1959.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Along with its sister papers <i><a href="/wiki/The_Observer" title="The Observer">The Observer</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian_Weekly" title="The Guardian Weekly">The Guardian Weekly</a></i>, <i>The Guardian</i> is part of the <a href="/wiki/Guardian_Media_Group" title="Guardian Media Group">Guardian Media Group</a>, owned by the <a href="/wiki/Scott_Trust_Limited" title="Scott Trust Limited">Scott Trust</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The trust was created in 1936 to "secure the financial and editorial independence of <i>The Guardian</i> in perpetuity and to safeguard the journalistic freedom and liberal values of <i>The Guardian</i> free from commercial or political interference".<sup id="cite_ref-GST1_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GST1-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The trust was converted into a <a href="/wiki/Limited_company" title="Limited company">limited company</a> in 2008, with a constitution written so as to maintain for <i>The Guardian</i> the same protections as were built into the structure of the Scott Trust by its creators. Profits are reinvested in journalism rather than distributed to owners or <a href="/wiki/Shareholder" title="Shareholder">shareholders</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GST1_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GST1-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The editor-in-chief <a href="/wiki/Katharine_Viner" title="Katharine Viner">Katharine Viner</a> succeeded <a href="/wiki/Alan_Rusbridger" title="Alan Rusbridger">Alan Rusbridger</a> in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian200315_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian200315-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RusbridgerFarewell_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RusbridgerFarewell-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Since 2018, the paper\'s main newsprint sections have been published in <a href="/wiki/Tabloid_format" class="mw-redirect" title="Tabloid format">tabloid format</a>. As of February 2020, its print edition had a daily circulation of 126,879.<sup id="cite_ref-circlead_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-circlead-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The newspaper has an online edition, <a href="/wiki/TheGuardian.com" title="TheGuardian.com">TheGuardian.com</a>, as well as two international websites, <i><a href="/wiki/Guardian_Australia" title="Guardian Australia">Guardian Australia</a></i> (founded in 2013) and <i><a href="/wiki/Guardian_US" title="Guardian US">Guardian US</a></i> (founded in 2011). The paper\'s readership is generally on the <a href="/wiki/Mainstream_left" class="mw-redirect" title="Mainstream left">mainstream left</a> of British political opinion,<sup id="cite_ref-International_Socialism_2003_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-International_Socialism_2003-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ipsos_MORI_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ipsos_MORI-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> and its reputation as a platform for <a href="/wiki/Social_liberal" class="mw-redirect" title="Social liberal">social liberal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Left-wing" class="mw-redirect" title="Left-wing">left-wing</a> editorial has led to the use of the "<i>Guardian</i> reader" and "Guardianista" as often-pejorative <a href="/wiki/Epithet" title="Epithet">epithets</a> for those of left-leaning or "<a href="/wiki/Politically_correct" class="mw-redirect" title="Politically correct">politically correct</a>" tendencies.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-What_the_papers_say_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-What_the_papers_say-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Guardian2003158_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian2003158-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Frequent <a href="/wiki/Typographical_error" title="Typographical error">typographical errors</a> during the age of <a href="/wiki/Typesetting#manual_typesetting" title="Typesetting">manual typesetting</a> led <i><a href="/wiki/Private_Eye" title="Private Eye">Private Eye</a></i> magazine to dub the paper the "Grauniad" in the 1960s, a nickname still used occasionally by the editors for self-mockery.<sup id="cite_ref-Marchi2019_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marchi2019-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In an <a href="/wiki/Ipsos_MORI" title="Ipsos MORI">Ipsos MORI</a> research poll in September 2018 designed to interrogate the public\'s trust of specific titles online, <i>The Guardian</i> scored highest for digital-content news, with 84% of readers agreeing that they "trust what [they] see in it".<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> A December 2018 report of a poll by the Publishers Audience Measurement Company (PAMCo) stated that the paper\'s print edition was found to be the most trusted in the UK in the period from October 2017 to September 2018. It was also reported to be the most-read of the UK\'s "quality newsbrands", including digital editions; other "quality" brands included <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Telegraph" title="The Daily Telegraph">The Daily Telegraph</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Independent" title="The Independent">The Independent</a></i>, and the <i><a href="/wiki/I_(newspaper)" title="I (newspaper)">i</a></i>. While <i>The Guardian</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s print circulation is in decline, the report indicated that news from <i>The Guardian</i>, including that reported online, reaches more than 23 million UK adults each month.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Chief among the notable "<a href="/wiki/Scoop_(news)" title="Scoop (news)">scoops</a>" obtained by the paper was the 2011 <a href="/wiki/News_International_phone_hacking_scandal" title="News International phone hacking scandal">News International phone-hacking scandal</a>\xe2\x80\x94and in particular the hacking of the murdered English teenager <a href="/wiki/Murder_of_Milly_Dowler" title="Murder of Milly Dowler">Milly Dowler</a>\'s phone.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The investigation led to the closure of the <i><a href="/wiki/News_of_the_World" title="News of the World">News of the World</a></i>, the UK\'s best-selling Sunday newspaper and one of the highest-circulation newspapers in history.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In June 2013, <i>The Guardian</i> broke news of the secret collection by the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Barack_Obama" title="Presidency of Barack Obama">Obama administration</a> of <a href="/wiki/Verizon_Communications" title="Verizon Communications">Verizon</a> telephone records,<sup id="cite_ref-reuters.com_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters.com-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> and subsequently revealed the existence of the surveillance program <a href="/wiki/PRISM_(surveillance_program)" title="PRISM (surveillance program)">PRISM</a> after knowledge of it was leaked to the paper by the <a href="/wiki/Whistleblower" title="Whistleblower">whistleblower</a> and former <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Agency" title="National Security Agency">NSA</a> contractor <a href="/wiki/Edward_Snowden" title="Edward Snowden">Edward Snowden</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-theguardian.com_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theguardian.com-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In 2016, <i>The Guardian</i> led an investigation into the <a href="/wiki/Panama_Papers" title="Panama Papers">Panama Papers</a>, exposing then\xe2\x80\x93Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a>\'s links to <a href="/wiki/Offshore_bank" title="Offshore bank">offshore bank accounts</a>. It has been named "newspaper of the year" four times at the annual <a href="/wiki/British_Press_Awards" class="mw-redirect" title="British Press Awards">British Press Awards</a>: most recently in 2014, for its reporting on government surveillance.<sup id="cite_ref-award_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-award-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#1821_to_1972"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">1821 to 1972</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-3"><a href="#Early_years"><span class="tocnumber">1.1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Early years</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-4"><a href="#Slavery_and_the_American_Civil_War"><span class="tocnumber">1.1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Slavery and the American Civil War</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-5"><a href="#C._P._Scott"><span class="tocnumber">1.1.3</span> <span class="toctext">C. P. Scott</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-6"><a href="#Spanish_Civil_War"><span class="tocnumber">1.1.4</span> <span class="toctext">Spanish Civil War</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-7"><a href="#Post-war"><span class="tocnumber">1.1.5</span> <span class="toctext">Post-war</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8"><a href="#1972_to_2000"><span class="tocnumber">1.2</span> <span class="toctext">1972 to 2000</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-9"><a href="#Northern_Ireland_conflict"><span class="tocnumber">1.2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Northern Ireland conflict</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-10"><a href="#Sarah_Tisdall"><span class="tocnumber">1.2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Sarah Tisdall</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-11"><a href="#Alleged_penetration_by_Russian_intelligence"><span class="tocnumber">1.2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Alleged penetration by Russian intelligence</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-12"><a href="#Jonathan_Aitken"><span class="tocnumber">1.2.4</span> <span War</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Since_2000"><span class="tocnumber">1.3</span> <span class="toctext">Since 2000</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-16"><a href="#Israeli-Palestinian_conflict_coverage"><span class="tocnumber">1.3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Israeli-Palestinian conflict coverage</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-17"><a href="#Clark_County"><span class="tocnumber">1.3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Clark County</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-18"><a href="#Guardian_America_and_Guardian_US"><span class="tocnumber">1.3.3</span> <span class="toctext"><i>Guardian America</i> and <i>Guardian US</i></span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 government</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-21"><a href="#Manafort\xe2\x80\x93Assange_secret_meetings"><span class="tocnumber">1.3.6</span> <span class="toctext">Manafort\xe2\x80\x93Assange secret meetings</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-22"><a href="#Priti_Patel_cartoon"><span class="tocnumber">1.3.7</span> <span class="toctext">Priti Patel cartoon</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-23"><a href="#WikiLeaks_coverage"><span class="tocnumber">1.3.8</span> <span class="toctext">WikiLeaks coverage</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Ownership_and_finances"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Ownership and finances</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#&quot;Membership&quot;_subscription_scheme"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">"Membership" subscription scheme</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Foundation_funding"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Foundation funding</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#Political_stance_and_editorial_opinion"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Political stance and editorial opinion</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#Circulation_and_format"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Circulation and format</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-29"><a href="#Publication_history"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Publication history</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-30"><a href="#Moving_to_the_Berliner_paper_format"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Moving to the Berliner paper format</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-31"><a href="#Reception"><span class="tocnumber">4.2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Reception</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-32"><a href="#Tabloid_format_since_2018"><span class="tocnumber">4.3</span> <span class="toctext">Tabloid format since 2018</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#Online_media"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Online media</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-34"><a href="#Podcasts"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Podcasts</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#GuardianFilms"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">GuardianFilms</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#References_in_popular_culture"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">References in popular culture</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-37"><a href="#Awards"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Awards</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-38"><a href="#Received"><span class="tocnumber">8.1</span> <span class="toctext">Received</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-39"><a href="#Given"><span class="tocnumber">8.2</span> <span class="toctext">Given</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-40"><a href="#Best_books_lists"><span class="tocnumber">8.3</span> <span class="toctext">Best books lists</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-41"><a href="#Editors"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Editors</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-42"><a href="#Notable_regular_contributors_(past_and_present)"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Notable regular contributors (past and present)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-43"><a href="#Guardian_News_&amp;_Media_archive"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Guardian News &amp; Media archive</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-44"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-45"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-46"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-47"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1821_to_1972">1821 to 1972</span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Manchester_Guardian,_May_5_1821.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/The_Manchester_Guardian%2C_May_5_1821.jpg/220px-The_Manchester_Guardian%2C_May_5_1821.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/The_Manchester_Guardian%2C_May_5_1821.jpg/330px-The_Manchester_Guardian%2C_May_5_1821.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/The_Manchester_Guardian%2C_May_5_1821.jpg/440px-The_Manchester_Guardian%2C_May_5_1821.jpg 2x" data-file-width="667" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Manchester_Guardian,_May_5_1821.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Manchester Guardian</i> Prospectus, 1821</div></div></div>\n<p><i>The Manchester Guardian</i> was founded in <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a> in 1821 by cotton merchant <a href="/wiki/John_Edward_Taylor" title="John Edward Taylor">John Edward Taylor</a> with backing from the <a href="/wiki/Little_Circle" title="Little Circle">Little Circle</a>, a group of <a href="/wiki/Nonconformist_(Protestantism)" title="Nonconformist (Protestantism)">non-conformist</a> businessmen.<sup id="cite_ref-G_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> They launched the paper after the police closure of the more <a href="/wiki/Radicalism_(historical)#Popular_agitation" title="Radicalism (historical)">radical</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Manchester_Observer" title="Manchester Observer">Manchester Observer</a></i>, a paper that had championed the cause of the <a href="/wiki/Peterloo_Massacre" title="Peterloo Massacre">Peterloo Massacre</a> protesters.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Taylor had been hostile to the radical reformers, writing: "They have appealed not to the reason but the passions and the suffering of their abused and credulous fellow-countrymen, from whose ill-requited industry they extort for themselves the means of a plentiful and comfortable existence. They do not toil, neither do they spin, but they live better than those that do."<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> When the government closed down the <i>Manchester Observer</i>, the mill-owners\' champions had the upper hand.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The influential journalist <a href="/wiki/Jeremiah_Garnett" title="Jeremiah Garnett">Jeremiah Garnett</a> joined Taylor during the establishment of the paper, and all of the Little Circle wrote articles for the new paper.<sup id="cite_ref-dnbGarnett_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnbGarnett-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The prospectus announcing the new publication proclaimed that it would "zealously enforce the principles of civil and religious Liberty [...] warmly advocate the cause of Reform [...] endeavour to assist in the diffusion of just principles of Political Economy and [...] support, without reference to the party from which they emanate, all serviceable measures".<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In 1825, the paper merged with the <i>British Volunteer</i> and was known as <i>The Manchester Guardian and British Volunteer</i> until 1828.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The working-class <i>Manchester and Salford Advertiser</i> called <i>The Manchester Guardian</i> "the foul <a href="/wiki/Prostitution" title="Prostitution">prostitute</a> and dirty <a href="/wiki/Parasite" class="mw-redirect" title="Parasite">parasite</a> of the worst portion of the mill-owners".<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Manchester Guardian</i> was generally hostile to labour\'s claims. Of the 1832 Ten Hours Bill, the paper doubted whether in view of the foreign competition "the passing of a law positively enacting a gradual destruction of the cotton manufacture in this kingdom would be a much less rational procedure."<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Manchester Guardian</i> dismissed strikes as the work of outside agitators: "[\xe2\x80\xa6]&#160;if an accommodation can be effected, the occupation of the agents of the Union is gone. They live on strife [...]."<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Slavery_and_the_American_Civil_War">Slavery and the American Civil War</span></h4>\n<p>The newspaper opposed slavery and supported <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a>. An 1823 leading article on the continuing "cruelty and injustice" to slaves in the <a href="/wiki/West_Indies" title="West Indies">West Indies</a> long after the abolition of the slave trade with the <a href="/wiki/Slave_Trade_Act_1807" title="Slave Trade Act 1807">Slave Trade Act 1807</a> wanted fairness to the interests and claims both of the planters and of their oppressed slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_1511_2012_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian_1511_2012-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> It welcomed the <a href="/wiki/Slavery_Abolition_Act_1833" title="Slavery Abolition Act 1833">Slavery Abolition Act 1833</a> and accepted the "increased compensation" to the planters as the "guilt of slavery attaches far more to the nation" rather than individuals. Success of the Act would encourage emancipation in other slave-owning nations to avoid "imminent risk of a violent and bloody termination."<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_0705_2011_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian_0705_2011-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> However, the newspaper argued against restricting trade with countries which had not yet abolished slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_2403_2012_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian_2403_2012-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Complex tensions developed in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Stoddard_2015_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stoddard_2015-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> When the abolitionist <a href="/wiki/George_Thompson_(abolitionist)" title="George Thompson (abolitionist)">George Thompson</a> toured, the newspaper said that "[s]lavery is a monstrous evil, but civil war is not a less one; and we would not seek the abolition even of the former through the imminent hazard of the latter". It suggested that the United States should compensate slave-owners for freeing slaves<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_1212_2008_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian_1212_2008-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> and called on President <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Pierce" title="Franklin Pierce">Franklin Pierce</a> to resolve the 1856 "civil war", the <a href="/wiki/Sacking_of_Lawrence" title="Sacking of Lawrence">Sacking of Lawrence</a> due to pro-slavery laws imposed by Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_1006_2015_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian_1006_2015-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1860, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Observer" title="The Observer">The Observer</a></i> quoted a report that the newly elected president <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> was opposed to abolition of slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-the_Guardian_1712_2014_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-the_Guardian_1712_2014-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> On 13 May 1861, shortly after the start of the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, the <i>Manchester Guardian</i> portrayed the Northern states as primarily imposing a burdensome trade monopoly on the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate States</a>, arguing that if the South was freed to have direct trade with Europe, "the day would not be distant when slavery itself would cease". Therefore, the newspaper asked "Why should the South be prevented from freeing itself from slavery?"<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_2305_2011_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian_2305_2011-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> This hopeful view was also held by the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal</a> leader <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Ewart Gladstone</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kettle_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kettle-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Abraham_Lincoln,_Lincoln_Square,_Manchester.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Abraham_Lincoln%2C_Lincoln_Square%2C_Manchester.jpg/220px-Abraham_Lincoln%2C_Lincoln_Square%2C_Manchester.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Abraham_Lincoln%2C_Lincoln_Square%2C_Manchester.jpg/330px-Abraham_Lincoln%2C_Lincoln_Square%2C_Manchester.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Abraham_Lincoln%2C_Lincoln_Square%2C_Manchester.jpg/440px-Abraham_Lincoln%2C_Lincoln_Square%2C_Manchester.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="3888" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Abraham_Lincoln,_Lincoln_Square,_Manchester.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Lincoln in Manchester, with extracts from the working men\'s letter and his reply on its base.</div></div></div>\n<p>There was division in Britain over the Civil War, even within political parties. The <i>Manchester Guardian</i> had also been conflicted. It had supported other <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Revolution" title="Age of Revolution">independence movements</a> and felt it should also support the rights of the Confederacy to self-determination. It criticised Lincoln\'s <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_Proclamation" title="Emancipation Proclamation">Emancipation Proclamation</a> for not freeing all American slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-Kettle_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kettle-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> On 10 October 1862, it wrote: "It is impossible to cast any reflections upon a man so evidently sincere and well-intentioned as Mr Lincoln but it is also impossible not to feel that it was an evil day both for America and the world, when he was chosen President of the United States".<sup id="cite_ref-Ayerst1971_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ayerst1971-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> By then, the <a href="/wiki/Union_blockade" title="Union blockade">Union blockade</a> was causing <a href="/wiki/Lancashire_Cotton_Famine#Politics" title="Lancashire Cotton Famine">suffering in British towns</a>. Some including <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a> supported the Confederacy as did  "current opinion in all classes" in London. On 31 December 1862, cotton workers held a meeting at the <a href="/wiki/Free_Trade_Hall" title="Free Trade Hall">Free Trade Hall</a> in <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a> which resolved "its detestation of negro slavery in America, and of the attempt of the rebellious Southern slave-holders to organise on the great American continent a nation having slavery as its basis". There was a comment that "an effort had been made in a leading article of the <i>Manchester Guardian</i> to deter the working men from assembling together for such a purpose". The newspaper reported all this and published their letter to President Lincoln<sup id="cite_ref-Rodrigues_2013_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rodrigues_2013-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> while complaining that "the chief occupation, if not the chief object of the meeting, seems to have been to abuse the <i>Manchester Guardian</i>".<sup id="cite_ref-Ayerst1971_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ayerst1971-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Lincoln replied to the letter thanking the workers for their "sublime Christian heroism" and American ships delivered relief supplies to Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-Rodrigues_2013_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rodrigues_2013-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The newspaper reported the shock to the community of the <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Abraham_Lincoln" title="Assassination of Abraham Lincoln">assassination of Abraham Lincoln</a> in 1865, concluding that "[t]he parting of his family with the dying President is too sad for description",<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_1402_2015_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian_1402_2015-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> but in what from today\'s perspective looks an ill-judged editorial wrote that "[o]f his rule we can never speak except as a series of acts abhorrent to every true notion of constitutional right and human liberty", adding "it is doubtless to be regretted that he had not the opportunity of vindicating his good intentions".<sup id="cite_ref-Kettle_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kettle-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Martin_Kettle" title="Martin Kettle">Martin Kettle</a>, writing for <i>The Guardian</i> in February 2011, "<i>The Guardian</i> had always hated slavery. But it doubted the Union hated slavery to the same degree. It argued that the Union had always tacitly condoned slavery by shielding the southern slave states from the condemnation they deserved. It was critical of Lincoln\'s emancipation proclamation for stopping short of a full repudiation of slavery throughout the US. And it chastised the president for being so willing to negotiate with the south, with slavery one of the issues still on the table".<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="C._P._Scott">C. P. Scott</span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/C._P._Scott" title="C. P. Scott">C. P. Scott</a> made the newspaper nationally recognised. He was editor for 57 years from 1872, and became its owner when he bought the paper from the estate of Taylor\'s son in 1907. Under Scott, the paper\'s moderate editorial line became more radical, supporting <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Gladstone</a> when the Liberals split in 1886, and opposing the <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Second Boer War</a> against popular opinion.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Scott supported the movement for <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women&#39;s suffrage">women\'s suffrage</a>, but was critical of any tactics by the <a href="/wiki/Suffragettes" class="mw-redirect" title="Suffragettes">Suffragettes</a> that involved <a href="/wiki/Direct_action" title="Direct action">direct action</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-suffragettes_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suffragettes-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> "The really ludicrous position is that <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">Mr Lloyd George</a> is fighting to enfranchise seven million women and the militants are smashing unoffending people\'s windows and breaking up benevolent societies\' meetings in a desperate effort to prevent him." Scott thought the Suffragettes\' "courage and devotion" was "worthy of a better cause and saner leadership".<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> It has been argued that Scott\'s criticism reflected a widespread disdain, at the time, for those women who "transgressed the gender expectations of <a href="/wiki/Edwardian_era" title="Edwardian era">Edwardian society</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-suffragettes_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suffragettes-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Scott commissioned <a href="/wiki/J._M._Synge" class="mw-redirect" title="J. M. Synge">J. M. Synge</a> and his friend <a href="/wiki/Jack_Yeats" class="mw-redirect" title="Jack Yeats">Jack Yeats</a> to produce articles and drawings documenting the social conditions of the west of Ireland; these pieces were published in 1911 in the collection <i>Travels in Wicklow, West Kerry and Connemara</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Scott\'s friendship with <a href="/wiki/Chaim_Weizmann" title="Chaim Weizmann">Chaim Weizmann</a> played a role in the <a href="/wiki/Balfour_Declaration_of_1917" class="mw-redirect" title="Balfour Declaration of 1917">Balfour Declaration of 1917</a>. In 1948 <i>The Manchester Guardian</i> was a supporter of the new State of <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>.\n</p><p>In 1919, the paper\'s special correspondent <a href="/wiki/William_Thomas_Goode" title="William Thomas Goode">W. T. Goode</a> travelled to Moscow and secured interviews with <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a> and other Soviet leaders.<sup id="cite_ref-Goode-BaW_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goode-BaW-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Guardian2011_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian2011-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ownership of the paper passed in June 1936 to the <a href="/wiki/Scott_Trust" class="mw-redirect" title="Scott Trust">Scott Trust</a> (named after the last owner, John Russell Scott, who was the first chairman of the Trust). This move ensured the paper\'s independence.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sylvia_Sprigge" title="Sylvia Sprigge">Sylvia Sprigge</a> served as correspondent for <i>The Manchester Guardian</i> in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> 1943\xe2\x80\x931953.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From 1930 to 1967, a special archival copy of all the daily newspapers was preserved in 700 zinc cases. These were found in 1988 whilst the newspaper\'s archives were deposited at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Manchester" title="University of Manchester">University of Manchester</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/John_Rylands_University_Library" class="mw-redirect" title="John Rylands University Library">John Rylands University Library</a>, on the Oxford Road campus. The first case was opened and found to contain the newspapers issued in August 1930 in pristine condition. The zinc cases had been made each month by the newspaper\'s plumber and stored for posterity. The other 699 cases were not opened and were all returned to storage at <i>The Guardian</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s garage, owing to shortage of space at the library.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish_Civil_War">Spanish Civil War</span></h4>\n<p>Traditionally affiliated with the centrist to centre-left <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a>, and with a northern, non-conformist circulation base, the paper earned a national reputation and the respect of the left during the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a> (1936\xe2\x80\x931939). <a href="/wiki/George_Orwell" title="George Orwell">George Orwell</a> writes in <i><a href="/wiki/Homage_to_Catalonia#Appendix_one" title="Homage to Catalonia">Homage to Catalonia</a></i> (1938): "Of our larger papers, the <i>Manchester Guardian</i> is the only one that leaves me with an increased respect for its honesty".<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> With the pro-Liberal <i><a href="/wiki/News_Chronicle" title="News Chronicle">News Chronicle</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a>-supporting <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Herald_(UK_newspaper)" class="mw-redirect" title="Daily Herald (UK newspaper)">Daily Herald</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Great_Britain" title="Communist Party of Great Britain">Communist Party</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Morning_Star_(British_newspaper)" title="Morning Star (British newspaper)">Daily Worker</a></i> and several Sunday and weekly papers, it supported the Republican government against General <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Franco" title="Francisco Franco">Francisco Franco</a>\'s insurgent nationalists.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war">Post-war</span></h4>\n<p>The paper\'s then editor, <a href="/wiki/A._P._Wadsworth" title="A. P. Wadsworth">A. P. Wadsworth</a>, so loathed Labour\'s <a href="/wiki/Left-wing" class="mw-redirect" title="Left-wing">left-wing</a> champion <a href="/wiki/Aneurin_Bevan" title="Aneurin Bevan">Aneurin Bevan</a>, who had made a reference to getting rid of "Tory Vermin" in a speech "and the hate-gospellers of his entourage" that it encouraged readers to vote Conservative and remove <a href="/wiki/Clement_Attlee" title="Clement Attlee">Attlee</a>\'s post-war Labour government.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The newspaper opposed the creation of the <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service" title="National Health Service">National Health Service</a> as it feared the state provision of healthcare would "eliminate selective elimination" and lead to an increase of congenitally deformed and feckless people.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>The Manchester Guardian</i> strongly opposed military intervention during the 1956 <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Suez Crisis</a>: "The Anglo-French ultimatum to Egypt is an act of folly, without justification in any terms but brief expediency. It pours petrol on a growing fire. There is no knowing what kind of explosion will follow."<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 24 August 1959, <i>The Manchester Guardian</i> changed its name to <i>The Guardian</i>. This change reflected the growing prominence of national and international affairs in the newspaper.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> In September 1961, <i>The Guardian</i>, which had previously only been published in <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a>, began to be printed in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1972_to_2000">1972 to 2000</span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Northern_Ireland_conflict">Northern Ireland conflict</span></h4>\n<p>When 13 civil rights demonstrators in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> were killed by British soldiers on 30 January 1972 (known as <a href="/wiki/Bloody_Sunday_(1972)" title="Bloody Sunday (1972)">Bloody Sunday</a>), <i>The Guardian</i> said that "Neither side can escape condemnation."<sup id="cite_ref-BloodySunday_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BloodySunday-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Of the protesters, they wrote, "The organizers of the demonstration, Miss <a href="/wiki/Bernadette_Devlin_McAliskey" title="Bernadette Devlin McAliskey">Bernadette Devlin</a> among them, deliberately challenged the ban on marches. They knew that stone throwing and <a href="/wiki/Sniping" class="mw-redirect" title="Sniping">sniping</a> could not be prevented, and that the <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army" title="Provisional Irish Republican Army">IRA</a> might use the <a href="/wiki/Human_shield" title="Human shield">crowd as a shield</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-BloodySunday_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BloodySunday-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Of the army, they wrote, "there seems little doubt that random shots were fired into the crowd, that aim was taken at individuals who were neither bombers nor weapons carriers and that excessive force was used".<sup id="cite_ref-BloodySunday_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BloodySunday-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many Irish people believed that the <a href="/wiki/Widgery_Tribunal" class="mw-redirect" title="Widgery Tribunal">Widgery Tribunal</a>\'s ruling on the killings was a whitewash,<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> a view that was later supported with the publication of the <a href="/wiki/Saville_inquiry" class="mw-redirect" title="Saville inquiry">Saville inquiry</a> in 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> but in 1972 <i>The Guardian</i> declared that "Widgery\'s report is not one-sided" (20 April 1972).<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> At the time the paper also supported <a href="/wiki/Internment" title="Internment">internment</a> without trial in Northern Ireland: "Internment without trial is hateful, repressive and undemocratic. In the existing Irish situation, most regrettably, it is also inevitable... .To remove the ringleaders, in the hope that the atmosphere might calm down, is a step to which there is no obvious alternative."<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Before then, <i>The Guardian</i> had called for British troops to be sent to the region: British soldiers could "present a more disinterested face of law and order,"<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> but only on condition that "Britain takes charge."<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sarah_Tisdall">Sarah Tisdall</span></h4>\n<p>In 1983 the paper was at the centre of a controversy surrounding documents regarding the stationing of <a href="/wiki/Cruise_missile" title="Cruise missile">cruise missiles</a> in Britain that were leaked to <i>The Guardian</i> by civil servant <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Tisdall" title="Sarah Tisdall">Sarah Tisdall</a>. The paper eventually complied with a court order to hand over the documents to the authorities, which resulted in a six-month prison sentence for Tisdall,<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> though she served only four. "I still blame myself," said <a href="/wiki/Peter_Preston" title="Peter Preston">Peter Preston</a>, who was the editor of <i>The Guardian</i> at the time, but he went on to argue that the paper had no choice because it "believed in the rule of law".<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> In an article discussing <a href="/wiki/Julian_Assange" title="Julian Assange">Julian Assange</a> and the protection of sources by journalists, <a href="/wiki/John_Pilger" title="John Pilger">John Pilger</a> criticised The Guardian\'s editor for betraying Tisdall by choosing not to go to prison "on a fundamental principle of protecting a source".<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alleged_penetration_by_Russian_intelligence">Alleged penetration by Russian intelligence</span></h4>\n<p>In 1994, <a href="/wiki/KGB" title="KGB">KGB</a> defector <a href="/wiki/Oleg_Gordievsky" title="Oleg Gordievsky">Oleg Gordievsky</a> identified <i>Guardian</i> literary editor <a href="/wiki/Richard_Gott" title="Richard Gott">Richard Gott</a> as "an agent of influence". While Gott denied that he received cash, he admitted he had had lunch at the Soviet Embassy and had taken benefits from the KGB on overseas visits. Gott resigned from his post.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gordievsky commented on the newspaper: "The KGB loved <i>The Guardian</i>. It was deemed highly susceptible to penetration."<sup id="cite_ref-findarticles.com_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-findarticles.com-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Jonathan_Aitken">Jonathan Aitken</span></h4>\n<p>In 1995, both the <a href="/wiki/Granada_Television" class="mw-redirect" title="Granada Television">Granada Television</a> programme <i><a href="/wiki/World_in_Action" title="World in Action">World in Action</a></i> and <i>The Guardian</i> were sued for <a href="/wiki/Libel" class="mw-redirect" title="Libel">libel</a> by the then cabinet minister <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Aitken#Libel,_arrest_and_prison" title="Jonathan Aitken">Jonathan Aitken</a>, for their allegation that <a href="/wiki/Harrods" title="Harrods">Harrods</a> owner <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_Al_Fayed" class="mw-redirect" title="Mohamed Al Fayed">Mohamed Al Fayed</a> had paid for Aitken and his wife to stay at the <a href="/wiki/H%C3%B4tel_Ritz_Paris" title="H\xc3\xb4tel Ritz Paris">H\xc3\xb4tel Ritz</a> in Paris, which would have amounted to accepting a bribe on Aitken\'s part. Aitken publicly stated that he would fight with "the simple sword of truth and the trusty shield of British fair play".<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The court case proceeded, and in 1997 <i>The Guardian</i> produced evidence that Aitken\'s claim of his wife paying for the hotel stay was untrue.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> In 1999, Aitken was jailed for <a href="/wiki/Perjury" title="Perjury">perjury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Perverting_the_course_of_justice" title="Perverting the course of justice">perverting the course of justice</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Connection"><i>Connection</i></span></h4>\n<p>In May 1998, a series of <i>Guardian</i> investigations exposed the wholesale fabrication of a much-garlanded ITV documentary <i>The Connection</i>, produced by <a href="/wiki/Carlton_Television#Factual_inaccuracy_in_1996_documentary" title="Carlton Television">Carlton Television.</a>\n</p><p>The documentary purported to film an undiscovered route by which heroin was smuggled into the United Kingdom from Colombia. An internal inquiry at Carlton found that <i>The Guardian\'</i>s allegations were in large part correct and the then industry regulator, the ITC, punished Carlton with a record \xc2\xa32-million fine<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> for multiple breaches of the UK\'s broadcasting codes. The scandal led to an impassioned debate about the accuracy of documentary production.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Later in June 1998, <i>The Guardian</i> revealed further fabrications in another Carlton documentary from the same director.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Kosovo_War">Kosovo War</span></h4>\n<p>The paper supported <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>\'s military intervention in the <a href="/wiki/Kosovo_War" title="Kosovo War">Kosovo War</a> in 1998\xe2\x80\x931999. <i>The Guardian</i> stated that "the only honourable course for Europe and America is to use military force".<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mary_Kaldor" title="Mary Kaldor">Mary Kaldor</a>\'s piece was headlined "Bombs away! But to save civilians, we must get in some soldiers too."<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Since_2000">Since 2000</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Guardian_senior_news_writer_Esther_Addley_interviewing_Ecuadorian_Foreign_Minister_Ricardo_Pati%C3%B1o.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" href="/wiki/File:The_Guardian_senior_news_writer_Esther_Addley_interviewing_Ecuadorian_Foreign_Minister_Ricardo_Pati%C3%B1o.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Guardian</i> senior news writer Esther Addley interviewing Ecuadorian foreign minister <a href="/wiki/Ricardo_Pati%C3%B1o" title="Ricardo Pati\xc3\xb1o">Ricardo Pati\xc3\xb1o</a> for an article relating to <a href="/wiki/Julian_Assange" title="Julian Assange">Julian Assange</a> in 2014.</div></div></div><p>\nIn the early 2000s, <i>The Guardian</i> challenged the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Settlement_1701" title="Act of Settlement 1701">Act of Settlement 1701</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Treason_Felony_Act_1848" title="Treason Felony Act 1848">Treason Felony Act 1848</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> In October 2004, <i>The Guardian</i> published a humorous column by <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Brooker" title="Charlie Brooker">Charlie Brooker</a> in its entertainment guide, the final sentence of which was viewed by some as a call for violence against U.S. President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a>; after a controversy, Brooker and the paper issued an apology, saying the "closing comments were intended as an ironic joke, not as a call to action."<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/7_July_2005_London_bombings" title="7 July 2005 London bombings">7 July 2005 London bombings</a>, <i>The Guardian</i> published an article on its comment pages by <a href="/wiki/Dilpazier_Aslam" title="Dilpazier Aslam">Dilpazier Aslam</a>, a 27-year-old British Muslim and journalism trainee from <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire" title="Yorkshire">Yorkshire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Aslam was a member of <a href="/wiki/Hizb_ut-Tahrir" title="Hizb ut-Tahrir">Hizb ut-Tahrir</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Islamist" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamist">Islamist</a> group, and had published a number of articles on their website. According to the paper, it did not know that Aslam was a member of Hizb ut-Tahrir when he applied to become a trainee, though several staff members were informed of this once he started at the paper.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Home_Office" title="Home Office">Home Office</a> has claimed the group\'s "ultimate aim is the establishment of an Islamic state (Caliphate), according to Hizb ut-Tahrir via non-violent means". <i>The Guardian</i> asked Aslam to resign his membership of the group and, when he did not do so, terminated his employment.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> In early 2009, the paper started a tax investigation into a number of major UK companies,<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> including publishing a database of the tax paid by the <a href="/wiki/FTSE_100" class="mw-redirect" title="FTSE 100">FTSE 100</a> companies.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Internal documents relating to <a href="/wiki/Barclays_Bank" class="mw-redirect" title="Barclays Bank">Barclays Bank</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Tax_avoidance" title="Tax avoidance">tax avoidance</a> were removed from <i>The Guardian</i> website after Barclays obtained a <a href="/wiki/Gagging_order" class="mw-redirect" title="Gagging order">gagging order</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> The paper played a pivotal role in exposing the depth of the <a href="/wiki/News_of_the_World_phone_hacking_affair" class="mw-redirect" title="News of the World phone hacking affair"><i>News of the World</i> phone hacking affair</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> <i><a href="/wiki/Intelligent_Life_(magazine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Intelligent Life (magazine)">Intelligent Life</a></i> magazine opined that... <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>As <a href="/wiki/Watergate_scandal" title="Watergate scandal">Watergate</a> is to the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">Washington Post</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Thalidomide" title="Thalidomide">thalidomide</a> to the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sunday_Times" title="The Sunday Times">Sunday Times</a></i>, so <a href="/wiki/News_International_phone_hacking_scandal" title="News International phone hacking scandal">phone-hacking</a> will surely be to <i>The Guardian</i>: a defining moment in its history.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Israeli-Palestinian_conflict_coverage">Israeli-Palestinian conflict coverage</span></h4>\n<p>In recent decades <i>The Guardian</i> has been accused of biased <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_the_Israeli_government" title="Criticism of the Israeli government">criticism of Israeli government policy</a><sup id="cite_ref-Sela_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sela-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> and of bias against the Palestinians.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> In December 2003, columnist <a href="/wiki/Julie_Burchill" title="Julie Burchill">Julie Burchill</a> cited "striking bias against the state of Israel" as one of the reasons she left the paper for <i>The Times</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Responding to these accusations, a <i>Guardian</i> editorial in 2002 condemned antisemitism and defended the paper\'s right to criticise the policies and actions of the Israeli government, arguing that those who view such criticism as inherently anti-Jewish are mistaken.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Harriet Sherwood, then <i>The Guardian</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> foreign editor, later its Jerusalem correspondent, has also denied that <i>The Guardian</i> has an anti-Israel bias, saying that the paper aims to cover all viewpoints in the <a href="/wiki/Israeli%E2%80%93Palestinian_conflict" title="Israeli\xe2\x80\x93Palestinian conflict">Israeli\xe2\x80\x93Palestinian conflict</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 6 November 2011, Chris Elliott, <i>The Guardian</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s readers\' editor, wrote that "<i>Guardian</i> reporters, writers and editors must be more vigilant about the language they use when writing about Jews or Israel," citing recent cases where <i>The Guardian</i> received complaints regarding language chosen to describe Jews or Israel. Elliott noted that, over nine months, he upheld complaints regarding language in certain articles that were seen as anti-Semitic, revising the language and footnoting this change.<sup id="cite_ref-Elliott_responds_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elliott_responds-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>The Guardian</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s style guide section referred to <a href="/wiki/Tel_Aviv" title="Tel Aviv">Tel Aviv</a> as the capital of Israel in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Guardian</i> later clarified: "In 1980, the Israeli Knesset enacted a law designating the city of Jerusalem, including East Jerusalem, as the country\'s capital. In response, the UN security council issued resolution 478, censuring the "change in character and status of the Holy City of Jerusalem" and calling on all member states with diplomatic missions in the city to withdraw. The UN has reaffirmed this position on several occasions, and almost every country now has its embassy in Tel Aviv. While it was therefore right to issue a correction to make clear Israel\'s designation of Jerusalem as its capital is not recognised by the international community, we accept that it is wrong to state that Tel Aviv \xe2\x80\x93 the country\'s financial and diplomatic centre \xe2\x80\x93 is the capital. The style guide has been amended accordingly."<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_retract_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian_retract-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 11 August 2014 the print edition of <i>The Guardian</i> published a pro-Israeli advocacy advert during the <a href="/wiki/2014_Israel%E2%80%93Gaza_conflict" class="mw-redirect" title="2014 Israel\xe2\x80\x93Gaza conflict">2014 Israel\xe2\x80\x93Gaza conflict</a> featuring <a href="/wiki/Elie_Wiesel" title="Elie Wiesel">Elie Wiesel</a>, headed by the words "Jews rejected child sacrifice 3,500 years ago. Now it\'s Hamas\' turn." <i>The Times</i> had decided against running the ad, although it had already appeared in major American newspapers.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> One week later, Chris Elliott expressed the opinion that the newspaper should have rejected the language used in the advert and should have negotiated with the advertiser on this matter.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Clark_County">Clark County</span></h4>\n<p>In August 2004, for the <a href="/wiki/2004_United_States_presidential_election" title="2004 United States presidential election">US presidential election</a>, the daily <i>G2</i> supplement launched an experimental letter-writing campaign in <a href="/wiki/Clark_County,_Ohio" title="Clark County, Ohio">Clark County, Ohio</a>, an average-sized county in a <a href="/wiki/Swing_state" title="Swing state">swing state</a>. Editor <a href="/wiki/Ian_Katz" title="Ian Katz">Ian Katz</a> bought a voter list from the county for $25 and asked readers to write to people listed as undecided in the election, giving them an impression of the international view and the importance of voting against President George W. Bush.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Wikipedia_and_sources_that_mirror_or_use_it" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="This claim cites another Wikipedia article. Articles need references to reliable third-party sources. (November 2020)">circular reference</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Katz admitted later that he did not believe Democrats who warned that the campaign would benefit Bush and not opponent <a href="/wiki/John_Kerry" title="John Kerry">John Kerry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rennie20041022_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rennie20041022-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> The newspaper scrapped "Operation Clark County" on 21 October 2004 after first publishing a column of responses\xe2\x80\x94nearly all of them outraged\xe2\x80\x94to the campaign under the headline "Dear Limey assholes".<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Some commentators suggested that the public\'s dislike of the campaign contributed to Bush\'s victory in Clark County.<sup id="cite_ref-bowers_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bowers-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Guardian_America_and_Guardian_US"><i>Guardian America</i> and <i>Guardian US</i></span></h4>\n<p>In 2007, the paper launched <i>Guardian America</i>, an attempt to capitalise on its large online readership in the United States, which at the time stood at more than 5.9 million. The company hired former <i><a href="/wiki/American_Prospect" class="mw-redirect" title="American Prospect">American Prospect</a></i> editor, <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_(magazine)" title="New York (magazine)">New York</a></i> magazine columnist and <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Review_of_Books" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Review of Books">New York Review of Books</a></i> writer <a href="/wiki/Michael_Tomasky" title="Michael Tomasky">Michael Tomasky</a> to head the project and hire a staff of American reporters and web editors. The site featured news from <i>The Guardian</i> that was relevant to an American audience: coverage of US news and the Middle East, for example.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Tomasky stepped down from his position as editor of <i>Guardian America</i> in February 2009, ceding editing and planning duties to other US and London staff. He retained his position as a columnist and blogger, taking the title editor-at-large.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In October 2009, the company abandoned the <i>Guardian America</i> homepage, instead directing users to a US news index page on the main <i>Guardian</i> website.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> The following month, the company laid off six American employees, including a reporter, a multimedia producer and four web editors. The move came as <i>Guardian News and Media</i> opted to reconsider its US strategy amid a huge effort to cut costs across the company.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> In subsequent years, however, <i>The Guardian</i> has hired various commentators on US affairs including <a href="/wiki/Ana_Marie_Cox" title="Ana Marie Cox">Ana Marie Cox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Wolff_(journalist)" title="Michael Wolff (journalist)">Michael Wolff</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naomi_Wolf" title="Naomi Wolf">Naomi Wolf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glenn_Greenwald" title="Glenn Greenwald">Glenn Greenwald</a> and George W. Bush\'s former speechwriter <a href="/wiki/Josh_Trevi%C3%B1o" class="mw-redirect" title="Josh Trevi\xc3\xb1o">Josh Trevi\xc3\xb1o</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Trevi\xc3\xb1o_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trevi\xc3\xb1o-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Trevi\xc3\xb1o\'s first blog post was an apology for a controversial tweet posted in June 2011 over the second Gaza flotilla, the controversy which had been revived by the appointment.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Guardian_US" title="Guardian US">Guardian US</a></i> launched in September 2011, led by editor-in-chief <a href="/wiki/Janine_Gibson" title="Janine Gibson">Janine Gibson</a>, which replaced the previous <i>Guardian America</i> service.<sup id="cite_ref-PressRel_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PressRel-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> After a period during which <a href="/wiki/Katharine_Viner" title="Katharine Viner">Katharine Viner</a> served as the US editor-in-chief before taking charge of <i>Guardian News and Media</i> as a whole, Viner\'s former deputy, Lee Glendinning, was appointed to succeed her as head of the American operation at the beginning of June 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gagged_from_reporting_Parliament">Gagged from reporting Parliament</span></h4>\n<p>In October 2009, <i>The Guardian</i> reported that it was forbidden to report on a parliamentary matter \xe2\x80\x93 a question recorded in a Commons order paper, to be answered by a minister later that week.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> The paper noted that it was being "forbidden from telling its readers why the paper is prevented\xe2\x80\x94for the first time in memory\xe2\x80\x94from reporting parliament. Legal obstacles, which cannot be identified, involve proceedings, which cannot be mentioned, on behalf of a client who must remain secret. The only fact <i>The Guardian</i> can report is that the case involves the London solicitors <a href="/wiki/Carter-Ruck" title="Carter-Ruck">Carter-Ruck</a>." The paper further claimed that this case appears "to call into question privileges guaranteeing free speech established under the <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_Rights_1689" title="Bill of Rights 1689">1689 Bill of Rights</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The only parliamentary question mentioning Carter-Ruck in the relevant period was by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Farrelly" title="Paul Farrelly">Paul Farrelly</a> MP, in reference to legal action by <a href="/wiki/Barclays" title="Barclays">Barclays</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trafigura" title="Trafigura">Trafigura</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> The part of the question referencing Carter-Ruck relates to the latter company\'s September 2009 gagging order on the publication of a 2006 internal report<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> into the <a href="/wiki/2006_C%C3%B4te_d%27Ivoire_toxic_waste_dump" class="mw-redirect" title="2006 C\xc3\xb4te d&#39;Ivoire toxic waste dump">2006 C\xc3\xb4te d\'Ivoire toxic waste dump</a> scandal, which involved a <a href="/wiki/Class_action" title="Class action">class action</a> case that the company only settled in September 2009 after <i>The Guardian</i> published some of the commodity trader\'s internal emails.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> The reporting injunction was lifted the next day, for Carter-Ruck withdrew it before <i>The Guardian</i> could challenge it in the High Court.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alan_Rusbridger" title="Alan Rusbridger">Alan Rusbridger</a> attributed the rapid back-down by Carter-Ruck to postings on Twitter,<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> as did a BBC article.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Edward_Snowden_leaks_and_intervention_by_the_UK_government">Edward Snowden leaks and intervention by the UK government</span></h4>\n<p>In June 2013, the newspaper broke news of the secret collection of <a href="/wiki/Verizon_Communications" title="Verizon Communications">Verizon</a> telephone records held by <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>\'s administration<sup id="cite_ref-reuters.com_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters.com-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> and subsequently revealed the existence of the <a href="/wiki/PRISM_(surveillance_program)" title="PRISM (surveillance program)">PRISM surveillance program</a> after it was leaked to the paper by former <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Agency" title="National Security Agency">NSA</a> contractor <a href="/wiki/Edward_Snowden" title="Edward Snowden">Edward Snowden</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-theguardian.com_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theguardian.com-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The newspaper was subsequently contacted by the British government\'s Cabinet Secretary, Sir <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Heywood" title="Jeremy Heywood">Jeremy Heywood</a>, under instruction from Prime Minister David Cameron and Deputy Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Nick_Clegg" title="Nick Clegg">Nick Clegg</a>, who ordered that the hard drives containing the information be destroyed.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Guardian</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> offices were then visited in July by agents from the UK\'s <a href="/wiki/Government_Communications_Headquarters" class="mw-redirect" title="Government Communications Headquarters">GCHQ</a>, who supervised the destruction of the hard drives containing information acquired from Snowden.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> In June 2014, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Register" title="The Register">The Register</a></i> reported that the information the government sought to suppress by destroying the hard drives related to the location of a "beyond top secret" internet monitoring base in <a href="/wiki/Seeb,_Oman" class="mw-redirect" title="Seeb, Oman">Seeb</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Oman</a>, and the close involvement of <a href="/wiki/BT_Group" title="BT Group">BT</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cable_%26_Wireless_plc" title="Cable &amp; Wireless plc">Cable &amp; Wireless</a> in intercepting internet communications.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Julian_Assange" title="Julian Assange">Julian Assange</a> criticised the newspaper for not publishing the entirety of the content when it had the chance.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Rusbridger had initially proceeded without the government\'s supervision, but subsequently sought it, and established an ongoing relationship with the <a href="/wiki/Defence_minister" title="Defence minister">Defence Ministry</a>. <i>The Guardian</i> enquiry later continued because the information had already been copied outside the United Kingdom, earning the newspaper a <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize" title="Pulitzer Prize">Pulitzer Prize</a>. Rusbridger and subsequent chief editors would sit on the government\'s <a href="/wiki/DSMA-Notice" title="DSMA-Notice">DSMA-notice</a> board.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Manafort.E2.80.93Assange_secret_meetings"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Manafort\xe2\x80\x93Assange_secret_meetings">Manafort\xe2\x80\x93Assange secret meetings</span></h4>\n<p>In a November 2018 <i>Guardian</i> article, <a href="/wiki/Luke_Harding" title="Luke Harding">Luke Harding</a> and Dan Collyns cited anonymous sources which stated that <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a>\'s former campaign manager <a href="/wiki/Paul_Manafort" title="Paul Manafort">Paul Manafort</a> held secret meetings with <a href="/wiki/WikiLeaks" title="WikiLeaks">WikiLeaks</a> founder <a href="/wiki/Julian_Assange" title="Julian Assange">Julian Assange</a> inside the Ecuadorian embassy in London in 2013, 2015, and 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> One reporter characterized the story, "If it\'s right, it might be the biggest get this year. If it\'s wrong, it might be the biggest gaffe." Manafort and Assange both denied ever having met with the latter threatening legal action against <i>The Guardian</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Ecuador\'s London consul Fidel Narv\xc3\xa1ez, who had worked at <a href="/wiki/Embassy_of_Ecuador,_London" title="Embassy of Ecuador, London">Ecuador\'s embassy in London</a> from 2010 to July 2018, denied that Manafort\'s visits had happened.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Priti_Patel_cartoon">Priti Patel cartoon</span></h4>\n<p>The Guardian was accused of being "racist and misogynistic" after it published a cartoon depicting <a href="/wiki/Home_Secretary" title="Home Secretary">Home Secretary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Priti_Patel" title="Priti Patel">Priti Patel</a> as a cow with a ring in its nose in an alleged reference to her <a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> faith, since cows are considered sacred in <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="WikiLeaks_coverage">WikiLeaks coverage</span></h4>\n<p>Journalist <a href="/wiki/Glenn_Greenwald" title="Glenn Greenwald">Glenn Greenwald</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Intercept" title="The Intercept">The Intercept</a></i>, a former contributor to <i>The Guardian</i>, has accused <i>The Guardian</i> of falsifying the words of <a href="/wiki/WikiLeaks" title="WikiLeaks">WikiLeaks</a> founder <a href="/wiki/Julian_Assange" title="Julian Assange">Julian Assange</a> in a report about the interview he gave to Italian newspaper <i>La Repubblica.</i> Greenwald wrote: "This article is about how those [<i>Guardian</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span>] false claims\xe2\x80\x94fabrications, really\xe2\x80\x94were spread all over the internet by journalists, causing hundreds of thousands of people (if not millions) to consume false news."<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Guardian</i> later amended its article about Assange.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (September 2019)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>After publishing a story on 13 January 2017 claiming that <a href="/wiki/WhatsApp" title="WhatsApp">WhatsApp</a> had a "backdoor [that] allows snooping on messages", more than 70 professional cryptographers signed on to an open letter calling for <i>The Guardian</i> to retract the article.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> On 13 June 2017, editor Paul Chadwick released an article detailing the flawed reporting in the original January article, which was amended to remove references to a backdoor.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ownership_and_finances">Ownership and finances</span></h2>\n<p><i>The Guardian</i> is part of the <a href="/wiki/Guardian_Media_Group" title="Guardian Media Group">Guardian Media Group</a> (GMG) of newspapers, radio stations and print media. GMG components include <i><a href="/wiki/The_Observer" title="The Observer">The Observer</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian_Weekly" title="The Guardian Weekly">The Guardian Weekly</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/TheGuardian.com" title="TheGuardian.com">TheGuardian.com</a></i>. All the aforementioned were owned by <a href="/wiki/Scott_Trust_Limited" title="Scott Trust Limited">The Scott Trust</a>, a charitable foundation existing between 1936 and 2008, which aimed to ensure the paper\'s <a href="/wiki/Editorial_independence" title="Editorial independence">editorial independence</a> in perpetuity, maintaining its financial health in order to ensure it did not become vulnerable to takeovers by for-profit media groups. At the beginning of October 2008, the Scott Trust<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s assets were transferred to a new limited company, The Scott Trust Limited, with the intention being that the original trust would be wound up.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Dame <a href="/wiki/Liz_Forgan" title="Liz Forgan">Liz Forgan</a>, chair of the Scott Trust, reassured staff that the purposes of the new company remained the same as under the previous arrangements.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Guardian_Building_Window_in_London.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/The_Guardian_Building_Window_in_London.JPG/220px-The_Guardian_Building_Window_in_London.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/The_Guardian_Building_Window_in_London.JPG/330px-The_Guardian_Building_Window_in_London.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/The_Guardian_Building_Window_in_London.JPG/440px-The_Guardian_Building_Window_in_London.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3170" data-file-height="1940" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Guardian_Building_Window_in_London.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Guardian</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> headquarters in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a></div></div></div>\n<p><i>The Guardian</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> ownership by the Scott Trust is probably a factor in its being the only British national daily to conduct (since 2003) an annual social, ethical and environmental <a href="/wiki/Audit" title="Audit">audit</a> in which it examines, under the scrutiny of an independent external auditor, its own behaviour as a company.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> It is also the only British national daily newspaper to employ an internal ombudsman (called the "readers\' editor") to handle complaints and corrections.\n</p><p><i>The Guardian</i> and its parent groups participate in <a href="/wiki/Project_Syndicate" title="Project Syndicate">Project Syndicate</a> and intervened in 1995 to
